p^{-j}} >= e\n  D0-  = margin-relaxed:      #{j : {m/p^j} >= 1/2 - p^{-j}} >= e\n\nClaimed sandwich (to verify exactly on data):\n  (S1)  n in D0  and  n-1 in D0+   ==>   n(n-1) | C(2n,n)     [lower inclusion]\n  (S2)  n(n-1) | C(2n,n)           ==>   n in D0 and n-1 in D0-  [upper inclusion]\n\nMechanism: q | n-1  =>  {n/q^j} = {(n-1)/q^j} + q^{-j} for all j with q^j <= ...\n(since n = (n-1)+1 and q^j | ... no wait: exact statement: n/q^j = (n-1)/q^j + q^{-j},\nso fractional parts differ by exactly q^{-j} modulo 1, with wraparound iff\n{(n-1)/q^j} >= 1 - q^{-j}.)\n\nAlso verify densities: D0 vs D0+ vs D0- should be near-identical.\n\"\"\"\nimport sys, math\nsys.path.insert(0, \"/home/claude/erdos396\")\nfrom divcheck import factorize_td, check\n\ndef carries_variant(m, variant):\n    \"\"\"Check m's own governor condition under margin variant: 'std', 'plus', 'minus'.\n    Condition: for each p^e || m, count of qualifying j >= e.\"\"\"\n    if m <= 1:\n        return True\n    for p, e in factorize_td(m).items():\n        cnt = 0\n        q = p\n        # j ranges over q = p^j <= 2m (carries beyond have {m/p^j} = m/p^j < 1/2)\n        while q <= 2*m:\n            r = m % q  # {m/q} = r/q\n            if variant == 'std':\n                ok = 2*r >= q\n            elif variant == 'plus':\n                ok = (2*r >= q) and (r < q - 1)      # {.} in [1/2, 1 - 1/q^j)\n            else:  # minus\n                ok = (2*r >= q - 2)                   # {.} >= 1/2 - 1/q^j\n            if ok:\n                cnt += 1\n            q *= p\n        if cnt < e:\n            return False\n    return True\n\nX = 60000\nviol_S1 = viol_S2 = 0\ncnt = {'D0':0, 'D0p':0, 'D0m':0, 'W1':0, 'S1pred':0}\nfor n in range(3, X+1):\n    w1 = check(n, 1)\n    d0_n   = carries_variant(n, 'std')\n    d0p_nm1 = carries_variant(n-1, 'plus')\n    d0m_nm1 = carries_variant(n-1, 'minus')\n    if d0_n: cnt['D0'] += 1\n    if carries_variant(n, 'plus'): cnt['D0p'] += 1\n    if carries_variant(n, 'minus'): cnt['D0m'] += 1\n    if w1: cnt['W1'] += 1\n    if d0_n and d0p_nm1:\n        cnt['S1pred'] += 1\n        if not w1:\n            viol_S1 += 1\n            if viol_S1 <= 5: print(f\"S1 VIOLATION at n={n}\")\n    if w1 and not (d0_n and d0m_nm1):\n        viol_S2 += 1\n        if viol_S2 <= 5: print(f\"S2 VIOLATION at n={n}\")\n\nprint(f\"\\nn <= {X}:\")\nprint(f\"  S1 violations: {viol_S1}   S2 violations: {viol_S2}\")\nprint(f\"  |D0|={cnt['D0']} ({cnt['D0']/X:.5f})  |D0+|={cnt['D0p']}  |D0-|={cnt['D0m']}\")\nprint(f\"  |W1|={cnt['W1']} ({cnt['W1']/X:.5f})\")\nprint(f\"  |D0 cap (D0+ +1)| = {cnt['S1pred']} ({cnt['S1pred']/X:.5f})  <- lower bound proxy for W1\")\nprint(f\"  sandwich tightness: W1 captured by lower set: {cnt['S1pred']/cnt['W1']:.4f}\")\nEOF\npython3 lemma0_test.py",

 "description": "The k=1 proof skeleton: theorem statements, exact reduction, lemma decomposition with status tags, Fourier reductions, failed approaches, risk register, and falsification tests",
 "file_text": "# Proof skeleton: positive logarithmic density for the $k=1$ window\n\n**Target.** $W_1 := \\{n\\ge 2 : n(n-1) \\mid \\binom{2n}{n}\\}$.\n\n**Theorem 1 (main target).** $W_1$ has logarithmic density $c_1^2$, where\n$c_1 = 0.11424\\ldots$ is the Ford–Konyagin constant (density of the governor\nset $D_0=\\{m: m\\mid\\binom{2m}{m}\\}$).\n\n**Theorem 1′ (acceptable weakening).** $W_1$ has positive lower logarithmic\ndensity. (Suffices for infinitude.)\n\n**Theorem 1″ (fallback).** Positive density at almost all scales\n(Tao–Teräväinen sense). (Still gives infinitude.)\n\n*Context.* The $k=1$ instance of Erdős 396 (\"exists $n$\") is trivial ($n=2$).\nThe value here is (i) the first result on the hard side of 396 beyond single\nshifts (Pomerance's $\\,n-k\\mid\\binom{2n}{n}$, $\\gg x/\\log^2x$ witnesses), and\n(ii) the template for $k\\ge2$, where each new factor adds one more point to\nthe correlation.\n\nStatus tags: **[K]** known/citable, **[KA]** known-adapt (low risk),\n**[N]** new but standard-toolbox (medium risk), **[HC]** hard core\n(make-or-break).\n\n---\n\n## §0. Notation and exact criterion\n\nFor prime $p$ and $m\\ge1$: $\\kappa_p(m) := \\#\\{j\\ge1 : \\{m/p^j\\}\\ge 1/2\\}$\n(= carries in $m+m$ base $p$ = $v_p\\binom{2m}{m}$, Kummer). Then\n$$n\\in W_1 \\iff \\forall p:\\ v_p\\big(n(n-1)\\big)\\le \\kappa_p(n).$$\nEvery prime divides at most one of $n,n-1$, so the conditions split by side:\nfor $p^e\\,\\|\\,n$ we need $e$ carries of $n$ at $p$; for $q^e\\,\\|\\,n-1$ we need\n$e$ carries **of $n$** at $q$.\n\nMargin variants of the governor set: for $\\sigma\\in\\{+,0,-\\}$ let\n$$\\kappa^{(\\sigma)}_p(m) := \\#\\Big\\{j\\ge1: \\{m/p^j\\}\\in I^{(\\sigma)}_j\\Big\\},\\quad\nI^{(+)}_j=[\\tfrac12,\\,1-p^{-j}),\\ I^{(0)}_j=[\\tfrac12,1),\\ I^{(-)}_j=[\\tfrac12-p^{-j},\\,1),$$\nand $D_0^{(\\sigma)} := \\{m : \\forall p^e\\|m,\\ \\kappa^{(\\sigma)}_p(m)\\ge e\\}$,\nso $D_0^{(0)}=D_0$.\n\n## §1. Lemma 0 — exact reduction to a two-set correlation **[verified]**\n\n**Lemma 0 (sandwich).**\n$$D_0\\cap\\big(D_0^{(+)}+1\\big)\\ \\subseteq\\ W_1\\ \\subseteq\\ D_0\\cap\\big(D_0^{(-)}+1\\big).$$\n\n*Proof.* If $q\\mid n-1$ then $n/q^j=(n-1)/q^j+q^{-j}$, so\n$\\{n/q^j\\}=\\{(n-1)/q^j\\}+q^{-j}$ unless $\\{(n-1)/q^j\\}\\ge 1-q^{-j}$ (wrap).\nHence $\\{(n-1)/q^j\\}\\in[\\tfrac12,1-q^{-j})\\Rightarrow\\{n/q^j\\}\\ge\\tfrac12$,\ngiving the left inclusion (the $p\\mid n$ conditions are verbatim $n\\in D_0$).\nConversely $\\{n/q^j\\}\\ge\\tfrac12\\Rightarrow\\{(n-1)/q^j\\}\\ge\\tfrac12-q^{-j}$,\ngiving the right inclusion. $\\square$\n\n**Lemma 0′ (margins are free).** $d(D_0^{(\\pm)})=d(D_0)=c_1$.\n*Sketch:* the symmetric difference forces some $\\{m/p^j\\}$ within $p^{-j}$ of\n$\\tfrac12$ or $1$ at a prime power relevant to $m$'s factorization; summing\n$p^{-j}\\cdot p^{-1}$-type costs over the FK decomposition gives density $0$.\n**[KA]** — routine within FK's framework.\n\n**Empirical verification.** Both inclusions hold with **zero exceptions** for\nall $n\\le 60{,}000$; $|D_0^{(+)}|=|D_0|$ exactly on this range and\n$|D_0^{(-)}|$ is $2\\%$ larger; the lower set captures $98.7\\%$ of $W_1$\n(`lemma0_test.py`).\n\n**Consequence.** Theorem 1 is *equivalent* to:\n$$\\boxed{\\ \\delta^{\\log}\\Big(D_0\\cap(D_0^{(\\pm)}+1)\\Big)=c_1^2\\ }$$\n— a pure binary correlation of two fixed, density-$c_1$ sets, each defined by\n(anatomy, digit) data of a single integer. All windowing/shift bookkeeping is\nnow gone, exactly (not just up to density-0 sets as in Pomerance's\n$D_0+k\\simeq D_{-k}$).\n\n## §2. Architecture\n\nWrite $\\mathbf 1_{D_0}(m) = \\Phi(\\mathcal A(m), \\mathcal R(m))$ where\n$\\mathcal A(m)$ = anatomy data (the sizes $u_i=\\log p_i/\\log m$ of the prime\nfactors $>m^{\\delta}$, with multiplicity) and $\\mathcal R(m)$ = digit data\n(residues $m \\bmod p_i^{j}$). The proof is a conditional-independence cascade:\n\n$$\\Pr^{\\log}\\big[n\\in D_0,\\ n-1\\in D_0^{(+)}\\big]\n= \\underbrace{\\Pr[\\mathcal A(n)\\in\\cdot\\,]\\Pr[\\mathcal A(n-1)\\in\\cdot\\,]}_{\\text{Lemma B}}\n\\times \\underbrace{\\Pr[\\mathcal R(n)\\ \\text{good}\\mid \\mathcal A]}_{\\text{Lemma C}}\n\\times \\underbrace{\\Pr[\\mathcal R(n-1)\\ \\text{good}\\mid \\text{rest}]}_{\\text{Lemma D}}\n+ o(1),$$\npreceded by Lemma A removing small primes. Lemmas A–C are [KA]/[N];\nLemma D is the hard core [HC].\n\n## §3. Lemma A — small primes ignorable, two-sided **[KA]**\n\n**Statement.** For $0<\\delta\\le1$, write $n(n-1)=A_nB_n$ with\n$P^+(A_n)\\le x^\\delta<P^-(B_n)$. Then\n$\\#\\{n\\le x: v_p(A_n)>\\kappa_p(n)\\ \\text{for some }p\\}\\ll x\\,e^{-c/\\delta}$.\n\n*Proof sketch.* FK Lemma 2.1 verbatim for $p^v\\mid n$ (low base-$p$ digits of\n$n$ are $0^v$; failure forces $<v$ of the remaining $D-v$ digits to be\n$\\ge p/2$; binomial digit-count). For $p^v\\mid n-1$: low digits of $n$ are\n$(1,0^{v-1})$, contributing no carries, and the identical counting applies to\nthe top $D-v$ digits. Union over the two sides and over $p\\le x^\\delta$, $v$,\nas in FK Prop. 1. Also discard $n$ with $p^2\\mid n(n-1)$ for some\n$p>x^\\delta$ (cost $\\ll x^{1-\\delta}$). After Lemma A: only squarefree\nlarge parts; each large prime needs $\\ge1$ carry.\n\n*Note (important negative result, §8):* the loss $e^{-c/\\delta}$ is a fixed\npositive density for fixed $\\delta$ — fine for asymptotics with\n$\\delta\\to0$ *after* $x\\to\\infty$, but it **forbids** any design that demands\nsmooth cofactors (smoothness probabilities $\\rho(1/\\delta)$-type always lose\nto $e^{-c/\\delta}$).\n\n## §4. Lemma B — binary independence of anatomies **[N, plausible via T18]**\n\n**Statement.** For finite unions of anatomy boxes\n$A,B\\subseteq\\{(u_1\\ge u_2\\ge\\cdots): \\sum u_i\\le 1\\}$,\n$$\\sum^{\\log}_{n\\le x}\\mathbf 1_{\\mathcal A(n)\\in A}\\,\\mathbf 1_{\\mathcal A(n-1)\\in B}\n= \\mathrm{PD}(A)\\,\\mathrm{PD}(B)+o(1).$$\n\n**Reduction to citable form (the $z$-trick).** Anatomy-box indicators are not\nmultiplicative, but: (i) the number of prime factors $>x^{1/3}$ is $\\le3$, so\n\"exactly $r$ primes in band $\\beta$, none in band $\\beta'$\" is recovered from\nfinitely many values of $z\\mapsto g_z(n):=\\prod_{p^a\\|n} z^{\\#\\{\\text{band-}\\beta\\ p\\}}\\mathbf 1_{p\\notin\\beta'}$\nby polynomial interpolation; each $g_z$ ($z\\in[0,1]$) is bounded, real,\nnon-negative, **multiplicative**, and equidistributed in APs to fixed moduli.\n(ii) Teräväinen's main discorrelation theorem [T18, Forum Math. Sigma 2018]\nthen gives each pairwise correlation $\\to$ product of mean values; his stated\napplications (independence of large-prime-factor counts of $n,n+1$;\nlogarithmic Erdős–Pomerance for consecutive smooth numbers) are exactly\ninstances of this. Work needed: (a) bands with $x$-dependent edges\n($p\\in(x^{a},x^{b}]$) — handle by approximating with finitely many fixed\nshapes and a continuity-in-PD argument; (b) bookkeeping for the $\\le3$\nband-prime constraint. Risk: low-medium; nothing conceptually new.\n\n## §5. Lemma C — digit conditions on the $n$-side, twisted **[N]**\n\n**Statement (FK twisted by a multiplicative weight).** For $g$ bounded\nmultiplicative (a Lemma-B-type weight carrying the $(n-1)$-anatomy condition),\nthe FK count of $n$ with prescribed large-prime data and digit successes,\nweighted by $g(n-1)$, factors as (FK main term)$\\times$(mean of $g$)$+o(1)$.\n\n**Where the new sums live.** FK detect, for $p\\,\\|\\,n$, $n=pm$, the conditions\n$\\{m/p^{a-1}\\}\\ge\\frac12$ by Fourier: $e(h\\,m/p^{a-1})$, then oscillate over\n$m$ and over the primes (their §§3–5; Lemma 3.2 handles phases\n$\\sum_a \\beta_a u^{-(a-1)}$ over $u=p$). Inserting $g(n-1)=g(pm-1)$ turns the\n$m$-sums into\n$$\\sum_{m\\sim x/p} g(pm-1)\\,e\\!\\big(h\\,m/p^{a-1}\\big),$$\ni.e. **mean values of bounded multiplicative functions in APs to modulus\n$p^{a-1}$** (the character is $m$-periodic mod $p^{a-1}$), needed *on average\nover $p\\sim x^{u}$*. Individual moduli of size up to $x^{1-u}$ are far beyond\nunconditional reach, but only the average over $p$ is needed —\nBombieri–Vinogradov-type theorems for multiplicative functions\n(e.g. Granville–Shao and successors) and/or large-sieve dispersion are the\ncandidate inputs. For Theorem 1′ (lower bound), one may also restrict the\n$a=2$ digit range so only $O(1)$ harmonics $h$ matter. Risk: medium; this is\nreal work but inside the standard toolbox.\n\n## §6. Lemma D — digit conditions on both sides **[HC]**\n\n**Statement.** With all anatomy fixed (Lemma B classes) and $n$-side digits\ndetected (Lemma C), the $(n-1)$-side digit conditions — by Lemma 0,\n*the carry conditions of $n-1$ at its own primes $q$* — equidistribute\njointly, i.e.\n$$\\sum^{\\log}_{n}\\Big[\\text{anat}\\Big]\\,\ne\\!\\Big(\\textstyle\\sum_{i,a}\\alpha_{i,a}\\frac{n}{p_i^a}\\Big)\\,\ne\\!\\Big(\\textstyle\\sum_{j,b}\\beta_{j,b}\\frac{n-1}{q_j^b}\\Big) = o(\\text{trivial}),\n\\qquad p_i\\mid n,\\ q_j\\mid n-1,$$\nfor nonzero frequency vectors.\n\n**Why it's the crux.** The two phase families live on coprime prime-power\nmoduli (good: CRT means no algebraic obstruction) but the summation variable\nparametrizes only one side multiplicatively at a time: writing $n=p m$ makes\nthe $p$-phases nice ($e(hm/p^{a-1})$) but turns the $q$-phases into\n$e(\\beta(pm-1)/q^b)$ — additive characters of modulus $q^b$ where $q$ is a\nprime factor of $pm-1$, **not a free variable**. This is the genuine binary\nentanglement.\n\n**Candidate attack D-i (dispersion).** Open the $q$-side via the divisor\nswitch: $n-1=q m'$, sum over the pairs of parametrizations subject to\n$pm - qm' = 1$, Cauchy–Schwarz in the smooth variables, and bound the\noff-diagonal bilinear sums\n$\\sum e\\big(n(\\beta/q^b-\\beta'/q'^b)\\big)$ by the curvature of\n$q\\mapsto q^{-b}$ (FK Lemma 3.2 is exactly a $j$-th-derivative-test engine for\nsuch phases). Classical dispersion à la Linnik/Motohashi/BFI; the required\nranges should be checked symbolically before any writing.\n\n**Candidate attack D-ii (logarithmic averaging / entropy decrement).** The\nTao–Teräväinen technology behind [T18] and the recent *\"Quantitative\ncorrelations and some problems on prime factors of consecutive integers\"*\n(Tao–Teräväinen) handles log-averaged binary correlations of objects with\nmultiplicative structure; the open question is whether their framework\ntolerates the digit factors $e(\\theta n/p^a)$, $p\\mid n$ — these are not\nmultiplicative, but they are *invariant enough* under the\n$n\\mapsto(\\text{remove }p)$ dilations that drive the method ($n=pm$:\nthe phase becomes a function of $m$ alone). First task: read that paper's\nhypotheses against this exact statement; it is possible Lemma D (or Theorem\n1″) is closer to citable than it looks.\n\n**Mitigations that shrink D's burden** (use freely for Theorem 1′):\n- *Automatic band:* if $q^2\\in(n,2n]$ (i.e. $q\\in(\\sqrt n,\\sqrt{2n}\\,)$), the\n  $j{=}2$ carry is free — top-prime-near-$\\sqrt n$ configurations need no\n  digit analysis at all.\n- *Deep digits:* primes with $u<1/3$ have $\\ge2$ chances; only an upper bound\n  on joint failure is needed, weakening the required equidistribution.\n- The truly unavoidable case is $u\\in(1/3,1/2)$ with its single coin flip —\n  Lemma D can be first proven for *one* such prime per side ($r=s=1$), which\n  already yields Theorem 1′ with a smaller constant.\n\n## §7. Assembly\n\nLemma A (with $\\delta\\to0$ last) + Lemma B (anatomy product) + Lemmas C, D\n(conditional digit products, FK §5 bookkeeping) + FK §7 (numerics of $c_1$)\n$\\Rightarrow$ Theorem 1. The PD computation of the limit constant is FK's\nverbatim, squared. Consistency data already in hand: measured pair density\n$=0.90$–$0.94\\times$(FK finite-scale density)$^2$ across $10^5$–$10^9$, drift\ndirection matching FK's own slow convergence.\n\n## §8. Failed approaches (recorded so effort isn't repeated)\n\n1. **Pure union bound / digit counting.** FK Lemma 2.1-type counting gives\n   unconditional per-prime failure bounds $\\ll(x/p)e^{-c/u}$, but the total\n   failure mass is $\\Theta(1)\\approx 0.99$ (the true density of $W_1$ is\n   $\\approx0.013$), so no union-bound design can reach positivity; the events\n   must be handled multiplicatively (inclusion–exclusion/independence). No\n   elementary proof by this route.\n2. **Smooth-cofactor designs.** Any event requiring a cofactor to be\n   $x^\\delta$-smooth costs $\\rho(c/\\delta)$-type probability, which always\n   loses to Lemma A's $e^{-c/\\delta}$ exceptional set. (This also retro-explains\n   why the Tier-B $n=m^2$ family underperformed.)\n3. **Semiprime trick (Pomerance Thm 3) on both sides.** Needs $n-1$ to be a\n   balanced semiprime along the sequence $\\{2pq-1\\}$; sieves give $P_2$ but\n   cannot unconditionally exclude the prime case, which is fatal\n   ($n-1$ prime $\\Rightarrow$ non-smooth $\\Rightarrow n\\notin W_1$).\n4. **Stable-set softness.** Even if $D_0$ were stable (unclear), Hildebrand '85\n   would give pairs — but McNamara's counterexample caps this route at pairs,\n   and $D_0$'s digit component likely breaks stability anyway. Worth a\n   10-minute check (is $D_0$ empirically stable under small dilations?), not\n   worth a proof attempt.\n\n## §9. Falsification tests to run next (cheap, before any proving)\n\n1. **Lemma B test:** measure $\\Pr[\\mathcal A(n)\\in A,\\ \\mathcal A(n-1)\\in B]$\n   vs product, for the specific band-boxes the proof uses, at $10^6$–$10^9$.\n2. **Lemma C/D test:** within fixed anatomy classes, measure the joint\n   distribution of the digit variables $(\\{n/p^2\\})_{p\\|n}$,\n   $(\\{(n-1)/q^2\\})_{q\\|n-1}$ — uniformity and cross-side independence,\n   with quantitative discrepancy estimates vs $x$.\n3. **Stability probe of $D_0$** (§8.4).\n4. **Margin robustness:** densities of $D_0^{(\\pm)}$ at $10^8$–$10^9$\n   (Lemma 0′ numerics).\n\n## §10. Beyond $k=1$\n\nThe same skeleton for general $k$ replaces Lemma B/D by $(k{+}1)$-point\ncorrelations: anatomy independence at order $\\ge3$ is open in natural density\n(and stable-set-impossible by McNamara), but the Tao–Teräväinen\nalmost-all-scales framework is the live route for $k=2$ (Theorem 1″-type\nconclusions still give infinitude, i.e. genuine cases of 396). ### TEXT
The skeleton is drafted, and it came out tighter than I expected, thanks to one new structural discovery made (and machine-verified) along the way:

**Lemma 0 — an exact sandwich.** Since $q\mid n-1$ shifts the base-$q$ digits of $n$ by exactly $q^{-j}$ in fractional-part terms, the carry conditions for $n$ at primes of $n-1$ are *the governor conditions of $n-1$ itself*, up to margins of width $q^{-j}$. So with margin-modified governor sets:
$$D_0\cap(D_0^{(+)}+1)\ \subseteq\ W_1\ \subseteq\ D_0\cap(D_0^{(-)}+1)$$
holds **exactly**, not just up to Pomerance's density-0 equivalence. I verified both inclusions with zero exceptions on 60,000 cases; the margins are free ($|D_0^{(+)}|=|D_0|$ on that range) and the lower set captures 98.7% of $W_1$. This turns the theorem into the cleanest possible statement: *the log-density of $D_0\cap(D_0+1)$ is $c_1^2$* — two fixed sets, one shift, no window bookkeeping.

The lemma decomposition with honest status tags:
- **Lemma A** [known-adapt]: two-sided "small primes ignorable" — FK's digit-counting adapts with one line changed (low digits $(1,0^{v-1})$ instead of $0^v$).
- **Lemma B** [new, plausible]: binary anatomy independence — reducible to Teräväinen's citable theorem via a $z$-interpolation trick I spelled out (band-anatomy indicators = finite combinations of bounded multiplicative functions, exploiting ≤3 primes above $x^{1/3}$).
- **Lemma C** [new, standard toolbox]: FK's exponential sums twisted by a multiplicative weight — lands on multiplicative functions in APs to large moduli *on average*, Bombieri–Vinogradov-for-multiplicative-functions territory.
- **Lemma D** [hard core]: joint digit equidistribution across both sides — the genuine bilinear entanglement, with two candidate attacks (dispersion; Tao–Teräväinen's quantitative-correlations framework, whose hypotheses should be read against this exact statement first) and three mitigations that shrink its burden for a lower-bound version, including the automatic-carry band near $\sqrt{n}$.

Equally important, **§8 records the dead ends with proofs of death**: union bounds can't work because total failure mass is $\Theta(1)$; smooth-cofactor designs always lose $\rho(c/\delta)$ vs $e^{-c/\delta}$ (which retroactively explains the Tier-B $m^2$ disappointment); the semiprime trick dies on excluding the prime case. That analysis cost real effort and shouldn't be repaid by a future session.

§9 lists the cheap falsification tests to run *before* attempting any proof — especially measuring the cross-side digit independence within fixed anatomy classes (a direct empirical test of Lemma D), and a quick stability probe of $D_0$.

My suggestion for next session: run the §9 tests, starting with the Lemma D falsification test — if the digit variables show unexpected cross-side correlation in the data, the skeleton needs revision before anyone invests in dispersion estimates; if they're clean, the next move is fetching the Tao–Teräväinen quantitative-correlations paper and checking whether Lemma D (or the almost-all-scales fallback) is already within its hypotheses.

### THINKING
 Now I'm moving forward with the falsification tests for §9, starting with Lemma D to check cross-side digit independence within fixed anatomy classes, followed by Lemma B, the $D_0$ stability probe, and margin robustness checks. Once those pass, I'll pull the Tao–Teräväinen quantitative correlations paper to verify whether Lemma D's hypotheses are actually covered there. The priority is measuring the joint distribution of digit variation within fixed anatomy classes.

For the Lemma C/D test, I'm sampling at scale ~10^8-10^9 and restricting to pairs where n has exactly one large prime p in the coin-flip band (u_p ∈ (1/3, 1/2)) and n-1 has exactly one large prime q in the same band. I'll measure the fractional parts {n/p²} and {(n-1)/q²} for uniformity, check whether both exceed 1/2 independently, and verify that anatomy values don't correlate with these distributions—expecting discrepancy to scale as N^{-1/2}. Then I'm testing Lemma B by comparing the joint distribution of anatomy classes across consecutive integers against the product distribution for specific band boxes. Finally, the $D_0$ stability probe checks whether membership in $D_0$ is preserved under multiplication by small integers d=2,3, expecting the set to be unstable since digits scramble under such operations.

For implementation, I'll either use vectorized trial division up to ~63k (the approach from extend_scale.py that handled 120k samples in 16 seconds) or sample fewer numbers and rely on sympy's factorint, which should complete ~400k factorizations in a couple of minutes. The expected disagreement rate for an independent set is around 0.20, which I'll measure empirically.

For the Lemma D test, I need to identify samples where n has exactly one prime factor in the range (n^{1/3}, n^{1/2}) and is sqrt-smooth. This class should represent roughly 4-6% of the full sample, so 200k samples would yield 8-12k qualifying numbers—enough to estimate probabilities within ±1% and cross-correlation precision around 1%.

Actually, I should loosen the classification: instead of requiring exactly one prime in that band, I'll take all primes of n falling in (n^{0.34}, n^{0.5}), which corresponds to the digit test condition where p² ≤ 2n < p³. For each such prime p dividing n, I compute the digit variable ξ_p = {n/p²}, and for cross-side analysis, I pair these with primes q dividing n-1 in the same band. Then I run tests on the marginal uniformity of ξ_p using KS statistics.

I'm also checking the expected value of the indicator 1_{ξ_p≥1/2} on each side, plus cross-side correlations between the indicators for p and q, comparing the joint probability to the product of marginals. Within a single side, if n has two band primes, I test whether their indicators are correlated. The sharpest test for Lemma D is whether the digit variable of n's prime depends on n-1's smoothness properties — checking if P(ξ_p ≥ 1/2 | n-1 sqrt-smooth) differs from the unconditional probability, which directly probes the conditioning concern.

For the digit-versus-anatomy test within a side, I'm checking P(ξ_p ≥ 1/2 | n/p smooth vs not), which relates to Lemma C. I'll run this on the [5e8, 2e9] range with 250k samples, using trial division by 6337 primes — roughly 30-40 seconds. After the tests, I need to pull the Tao–Teräväinen paper on quantitative correlations and consecutive integer factors from arXiv and compare my results against Lemmas B and D.

For the stability test, I'm checking whether the density of disagreement between n and dn is zero for each prime d. Testing d=2 specifically: I'll compute the fraction of n ≤ 10^6 where n and 2n disagree on membership in D_0. If that's around 0.2, it means the set isn't stable, which confirms §8.4 is a dead end and definitively rules out the Hildebrand approach.

Writing the test script now...

Defining the test structure... Now I'm checking the stability of D_0 across small values of d by measuring disagreement rates up to n = 3e5, then extending the membership check to 1.5e6 using a governor check with trial division. For Part 3, I need to compute the densities of D_0^{(±)} at 10^8 by sampling 100k values in [1e8, 2e8] and factoring them with trial division up to roughly 20000 primes — I'm weighing whether to vectorize this in numpy or keep it scalar, since the scale is manageable either way.

Actually, I can simplify this: since Part 1 already collects full factorizations at the 2e9 scale, I'll compute D_0 / D_0+ / D_0- membership directly from those factorizations and fold the margin analysis into Part 1 itself. This also lets me measure the core Theorem-1 quantities — P(n ∈ D_0), P(n-1 ∈ D_0+), and their joint probability versus the product — all at the same 2e9 scale with the sandwich bounds, giving a complete capstone result. I need to be careful computing carries since it requires all prime factors including small ones, but I have the full factorization from trial division plus the cofactor, so I can compute exact D_0 membership by checking κ_p(n) ≥ e for each prime power p^e dividing n.

For the carries computation, I'm looking at roughly 7-9 distinct primes per number times 250k numbers, which is about 2 million carries calls with ~15 operations each — totally manageable. The edge case is when the cofactor after dividing out primes up to 63246 is itself a large prime; if that cofactor exceeds sqrt(2n), then n automatically fails D_0 membership since no carry is possible. But I'll just treat the cofactor as a prime with exponent 1 and run the same carries check, which handles all cases correctly including when the cofactor is small enough to potentially contribute a carry.

Memory-wise, storing factor dictionaries for 250k samples with 2 entries each should be fine based on how extend_scale.py handled 120k×3. The trial-division loop over nonzero entries totals around 1.5M scalar operations, which is acceptable.

For timing, I'm looking at 6337 primes and a 500k-entry matrix, so roughly 3.2 billion elementwise modulo operations across all numpy passes. The previous extend_scale run did 2.3 billion operations in 16 seconds, so this should take around 22 seconds.

Next I'll write up the results, fetch and assess the Tao–Teräväinen paper, and update status tags as needed. I also want to add a correlation coefficient with error bars to the cross-side test. For the stability test, I'm planning a separate script that computes inD0(n), inD0(2n), and inD0(3n) for n up to 200,000 and tracks disagreement fractions—though the trial division approach will be slow with 600k membership checks.

e = 0\n        while x % p == 0:\n            x //= p; e += 1\n        cof[r, c] = x\n        fac[r][c][p] = e\nprint(f\"trial division done {time.time()-t0:.0f}s\", flush=True)\n\n\ndef full_factors(r, c):\n    f = dict(fac[r][c])\n    co = int(cof[r, c])\n    if co > 1:\n        f[co] = f.get(co, 0) + 1\n    return f\n\n\ndef governor(m, f, variant):\n    \"\"\"m in D0^(variant) given factorization f. variant in {'std','plus','minus'}.\"\"\"\n    for p, e in f.items():\n        cnt = 0\n        q = p\n        while q <= 2 * m:\n            r = m % q\n            if variant == 'std':\n                ok = 2 * r >= q\n            elif variant == 'plus':\n                ok = (2 * r >= q) and (r < q - 1)\n            else:\n                ok = 2 * r >= q - 2\n            if ok:\n                cnt += 1\n            q *= p\n        if cnt < e:\n            return False\n    return True\n\n\n# ---- collect per-sample data ----\nrecs = []\nfor r in range(N):\n    n = int(ns[r])\n    lo3 = round((2 * n) ** (1 / 3)); hi2 = math.isqrt(2 * n)\n    rec = {}\n    for c, m in ((0, n), (1, n - 1)):\n        f = full_factors(r, c)\n        Pp = max(f) if f else 1\n        band = sorted([p for p in f if lo3 < p <= hi2], reverse=True)\n        # digit data at band primes (digits OF n, per the window condition)\n        digs = []\n        for p in band:\n            p2 = p * p\n            rr = n % p2\n            digs.append((math.log(p) / math.log(n), rr / p2, 2 * rr >= p2))\n        rec[c] = {\"f\": f, \"Pmax\": Pp, \"smooth\": Pp <= hi2, \"band\": band, \"digs\": digs}\n    rec[\"D0_n\"] = governor(n, rec[0][\"f\"], 'std')\n    rec[\"D0p_nm1\"] = governor(n - 1, rec[1][\"f\"], 'plus')\n    rec[\"D0_nm1\"] = governor(n - 1, rec[1][\"f\"], 'std')\n    rec[\"D0m_nm1\"] = governor(n - 1, rec[1][\"f\"], 'minus')\n    recs.append(rec)\nprint(f\"classification done {time.time()-t0:.0f}s\", flush=True)\n\nout = {}\n\n# ---- T1: anatomy independence ----\nprint(\"\\n== T1: Lemma B (anatomy independence) ==\")\nevents = {\n    \"smooth(sqrt2n)\": lambda s: s[\"smooth\"],\n    \">=1 band prime\": lambda s: len(s[\"band\"]) >= 1,\n    \"P+ <= n^0.40\":   lambda s: s[\"Pmax\"] <= 0 or s[\"Pmax\"] <= math.exp(0.40 * math.log(2e9)),\n}\nfor name, ev in events.items():\n    a = np.array([ev(rec[0]) for rec in recs])\n    b = np.array([ev(rec[1]) for rec in recs])\n    pj = float(np.mean(a & b)); ### THINKING
 Looking at the test results now. The first test shows ratios very close to 1 with mild negative correlations, which aligns with expected finite-scale behavior—that passes without issue. But the second test is problematic: the success rate is 0.5254 instead of the expected 0.5000, and the chi-squared statistic for the digit marginals is enormous at 379 across 9 degrees of freedom, indicating a significant deviation.

Breaking this down by the u parameter reveals the culprit: most buckets are clean (0.5015 and 0.4951), but the range u ∈ [0.45, 0.52) shows an elevated success rate of 0.5769. This is the automatic-carry band—when p² falls between n and 2n, the fractional part {n/p²} automatically lands in (1/2, 1], guaranteeing success. Since this bucket captures both primes below √n (which behave like fair coin flips) and primes in the range (√n, √(2n)] (which always succeed), the mixture skews the observed rate upward. The automatics comprise roughly 24% of the log-measure in that bucket, so the predicted success rate should be around 0.62, though the measured 0.577 suggests the effect is somewhat dampened.

The same structural issue explains the chi² inflation in the xi distribution: primes with p² ∈ (n, 2n] produce xi values deterministically concentrated in (1/2, 1), which artificially inflates the upper deciles. To get clean, uniform behavior, I should restrict analysis to p² ≤ n (equivalently u < 1/2 exactly), where xi should distribute uniformly without this automatic-band contamination.

For T3, the mild negative deviation in the within-side ratio (0.952 ± 0.011) likely stems from mixture-induced correlation rather than true conditional dependence: the automatic band forces p1·p2 ≤ n, so when p1 is large, p2 must be small, creating anti-correlation in sizes that propagates through the u-values since success probability depends on u. Restricting both primes to p² ≤ n should eliminate this artifact and push the ratio toward 1.

T4's cross-side ratio (0.984 ± 0.010) sits cleanly within 2 SE of 1, and the sharp conditioning test shows P(success | n-1 smooth) = 0.5321 versus 0.5326 unconditional—essentially flat at 0.0005 difference against SE 0.0032—confirming Lemma D's independence empirically, though the same mixture caveat applies.

T5 confirms P(n ∈ D0) = 0.12308, matching FK's theoretical 0.1238 at 1e9 scale, with negligible cost difference at this resolution.

All tests pass with the two artifacts fully explained by the automatic band mixture. I need to run the cleaned version restricting to p ≤ sqrt(n) to properly validate T2/T3—I'll modify the script to output only the restricted stats, which should take about 40 seconds to rerun.

For the follow-up, I'll condition on u < 0.5 exactly and use only coin-flip primes for T3/T4, then add a KS-style decile chi² test for the restricted data. After that, I'll pull the Tao–Teräväinen quantitative correlations paper to check my hypotheses against Lemma B and D, then update the skeleton tests report with the new statuses and document how the automatic band mixture actually confirms the provable carries—plus record that Lemma D is now empirically clean.

### THINKING
All tests pass—the anomalies in T2/T3 are explained by the automatic-carry band (primes with $p^2\in(n,2n]$ that succeed deterministically), which is a known mitigation, not a violation. I'll re-run the tests restricted to true coin-flip primes to confirm.

### THINKING
 The T4 cross-side ratio is essentially perfect at 1.003, which validates the core independence assumption beautifully. But the n-side success rate is sitting at 0.4841 instead of the expected 0.5, running about 10 standard errors below, and the chi-squared value remains stubbornly high at 143. The within-side T3 ratio shows a similar pattern around 0.959, suggesting something systematic is pulling the results down.

I'm working through the math on why this might happen for primes in that range. If I model the residues as uniform, the success condition should actually predict slightly above 1/2, not below — so there's something else at play, likely a boundary effect when primes get close to sqrt(n), where the digit structure of n in base p starts to constrain the distribution in ways the uniform model doesn't capture. I'm realizing there might be a selection effect I missed: when p² divides n, the digit test automatically fails since n mod p² = 0, which means xi = 0. This could be skewing the results for primes in certain bands where p² | n is possible. The frequency of such primes scales roughly as the sum of reciprocals of p² for primes above (2n)^{1/3}, which could be creating a systematic bias in what I'm observing. I'm realizing the issue might be a boundary effect at the top digit. When n is uniformly distributed over a range and I'm looking at n mod p², the fractional part should be uniform across multiples of p—but only if the range is large enough relative to p². Let me check if the range of 1.5 billion samples is actually sufficient for this assumption to hold.

For p near the fourth root of n, p² becomes comparable to the range length itself, so the modular arithmetic doesn't equidistribute properly and boundary effects dominate. This would explain why the chi² test stays elevated near u = 0.5, matching what I'm seeing in the data buckets. There's a symmetric failure band just below sqrt(n): when p² falls in the range (2n/3, n], the value xi stays below 1/2, causing automatic failure. But when p² is in (n/2, 2n/3], it succeeds deterministically. So near the top of the range, these success and failure bands alternate in a predictable pattern based on the ratio p²/n. The deterministic slivers in the (n/2, 2n/3] range show success with log-measure 0.288, while the (2n/3, n] range fails with log-measure 0.405—creating a net negative bias when I exclude the automatic successes above n and focus only on these sub-sqrt deterministic regions. , n/2]: when the digit is 2, I need xi = n/p² - 2 to fall in [0,1), which succeeds only if p² ≤ 0.4n. The interval (n/3, 0.4n] favors success while (0.4n, n/2] fails, creating a slight negative bias that diminishes as the digit grows. These boundary effects match exactly what Flajolet-Kleitman's formula captures through the floor function in their free-digit count—the deficit isn't random noise but rather the deterministic fine structure encoded in how the floor function handles top-digit constraints.

The key insight is that I was testing the wrong position: the top digit's behavior is inherently deterministic given the fractional part of 1/u, while interior digits (strictly below the top) should follow the uniform distribution. For band primes where 1/3 < u < 1/2, the second digit is actually the top digit and partially deterministic, not truly random. The success probability isn't simply 1/2 but a piecewise function of the fractional position—exactly what FK's formula integrates over all primes.

The empirical evidence confirms this: the cross-side ratio of 1.003 ± 0.012 shows perfect independence between different prime pairs, while the within-side ratio of 0.959 ± 0.014 reveals a small systematic deviation that likely stems from the shared deterministic constraint on the top digit when both primes fall in the same range. I should test this by restricting to cases where both primes are strictly in the interior range and excluding the deterministic boundary effects. Now I need to fetch the test results and write up the report with status updates. The skeleton also needs a note clarifying that the per-prime success probability follows an explicit piecewise function based on top-digit determinism, and the equidistribution lemmas should be stated specifically for interior digits while handling the top digit deterministically — this is a key correction that emerged from testing.

So the issue is that m's range is far too short compared to p₁p₂ — when m spans only n^{0.05} to n^{1/3} but the primes multiply to n^{2/3} to n^{0.95}, the Chinese Remainder Theorem's equidistribution breaks down. The pairs (m mod p₁, m mod p₂) don't cover the full grid uniformly; instead they trace out a thin diagonal-like pattern, which means even the marginals fail to be properly uniform when p₁ approaches n. 1.5e9/(p₁p₂) ≤ p₁p₂ requires p₁p₂ ≤ 4e4, but the factorization constraint forces p₁p₂ ≥ 1e6, so the joint distribution of digit pairs is confined to a sparse one-dimensional arithmetic progression rather than filling the plane uniformly. The near-independence I'm seeing (0.96) emerges only when averaging across all prime pairs — the ensemble mixes many thin progressions together, and this is exactly what Friedlander-Iwaniec's Section 4 on simultaneous fractional parts addresses, which explains why that's the hardest part of the analysis.

The empirical deficit of ~4% appears real across three independent runs at similar scale (0.952, 0.959, 0.962), reaching about 5 standard errors jointly, but this finite-scale correlation shouldn't invalidate the skeleton since FK proved their theorem — any within-side correlation either vanishes asymptotically or is already accounted for in their main term, assuming digit independence across primes given the anatomical structure.

This means I need to revise the skeleton's section 6 remark about CRT and coprime moduli: while there's no algebraic obstruction, the counting obstruction is actually severe, and the equidistribution FK establishes is ensemble-level rather than pointwise. The cross-side ratios (1.003, 0.995) are notably cleaner than within-side, which suggests a structural difference in how the two behave. ### TEXT
This round executed all of §9's falsification tests plus the literature check on the new engine, and the skeleton survived with upgrades. The state of play:

**The hard core passed its empirical trial.** Lemma D's cross-side digit independence — the make-or-break assumption — reads 0.984, 1.003, 0.995 across three independent 250k-sample runs at $n\sim10^9$, and the sharpest probe (does $n$'s digit success care about $n{-}1$'s anatomy?) is flat to within 1σ. Notably, the cross-side coupling is *cleaner* than the within-side one — the $+1$ shift decorrelates better than the multiplicative link inside a single integer. A small empirical gift to Lemma D.

**Two apparent anomalies turned into structural understanding**, both now folded into the skeleton:
1. *Top-digit determinism*: for band primes the lone carry digit is the top digit, with an explicit piecewise deterministic law (forced success/failure in alternating size-ratio slivers). The raw data was fingerprinting Ford–Konyagin's $\lfloor 1/u\rfloor$ boundary terms; restricting to interior digits restored exactly 1/2 and collapsed the chi² from ~380 to ~15. Drafting consequence: Lemmas C/D should claim equidistribution only for interior digits.
2. *Equidistribution is ensemble-only*: for fixed primes the cofactor ranges are far too short for pointwise joint uniformity — which is precisely why FK's "simultaneous fractional parts" section is the analytic heart. My skeleton's "CRT, no obstruction" remark was too glib; corrected. The persistent ~4% within-side deficit (5σ combined) is this phenomenon at finite scale — already covered by FK's theorem asymptotically, but a benchmark any error bookkeeping must beat.

**The literature delivered a template at the perfect moment.** Tao–Teräväinen (Dec 2025) proved a joint $(n,n+1)$ statement via circle method → **Pilatte's** quantitative correlation estimates on minor arcs → sieve on major arcs — structurally the pipeline Lemma D needs, and our digit conditions are already frequency-side objects, so the circle-method encoding is native to our problem. Their stated technology frontier ("logarithmic averaging or almost all scales") is verbatim our Theorem 1/1″ dichotomy, which suggests Theorem 1″ (almost all scales — still gives infinitude) is the realistic first theorem. Lemma B's risk drops to low with Pilatte's engine named.

**One loose end closed for good**: $D_0$ is *not* Hildebrand-stable (dilation disagreement 0.14–0.18 vs the required ~0), so the soft route is dead even for pairs — the skeleton is the only road, now confirmed rather than assumed.

The fork ahead: (a) the **reading session** — fetch Pilatte's paper and Tao–Teräväinen's general two-point estimate, and check its hypotheses line-by-line against our $z$-trick functions and the cofactor-invariance structure (this determines whether Lemma B is citable and how far D-ii reaches); (b) start the **manuscript** with the fully-specified pieces (Lemma 0/0′ and Lemma A, ~2–3 pages, zero research risk); or (c) the **$k\ge2$ track** — compute Balog–Wooley witnesses for small parameters and measure their carry profiles. My recommendation is (a), since its outcome reshapes how (b) gets written.

### THINKING
 I found a paper by Pilatte on improved bounds for the two-point logarithmic Chowla conjecture, which establishes a power-of-log saving over the trivial bound for log-averaged two-point correlations of the Liouville function—specifically showing a saving of $(\log x)^{-c}$ for some absolute constant $c > 0$.

The key insight from Pilatte's work is that multiplicativity of the Liouville function is only needed at one step (Theorem 2.4), while the rest of the argument only requires that it's bounded by 1. This modularity is important because it suggests the method could generalize beyond multiplicative functions.

Now I'm looking at how a more recent paper (TT 2512.01739) extends Pilatte's approach to handle more general functions—both real-valued equidistributed ones and complex-valued non-pretentious ones—through their Theorem 3.1, which I need to examine more carefully. I'll try fetching Pilatte's paper with a modest token limit to extract Theorem 1.1 and understand the key structural requirements, then fetch the TT paper's HTML with a higher limit to hopefully capture Theorem 3.1 in the early sections.

Once I have both statements, I'll build out the line-by-line analysis in HYPOTHESES_CHECK.md, checking whether Lemma B's conclusion about the correlation sum—with its error term savings and the equidistribution condition on the multiplicative functions—aligns with what TT Theorem 3.1 actually claims. The mean value along non-principal characters vanishes since g_z behaves like 1 rather than χ, confirming equidistribution with proper quantitative bounds. There's a potential issue though: Pilatte and TT's theorem might assume fixed functions while ours shift with x (the bands scale as x^α), so I need to verify their estimates are uniform enough across this dependence — that's a key verification point. Their results use logarithmic averaging primarily, with natural averaging covered in remarks, which aligns with what I need for Theorem 1.

Now I'm checking whether the correlation estimates actually give the asymptotic form with the product of means as the main term, not just o(1) discorrelation of mean-zero functions. TT's statement should have the shape: correlation minus the product of individual means is bounded by some savings term, which matches the qualitative structure from T18. I need to confirm TT 3.1 has this quantitative version.

There's also a structural issue: the digit factor with the exponential and the divisibility indicator isn't multiplicative, so I need to trace through Pilatte's argument to see exactly where multiplicativity of λ is invoked and whether the rest of the machinery can work without it.

The key reduction step is where Thm 1.1 gets reduced to Thm 2.4 by averaging over products of many primes—this is the Tao-style entropy decrement where λ(n)λ(n+1) gets replaced using the multiplicative identity λ(pn) = λ(p)λ(n). For our indicator function on digit sets, when we dilate n to pn with a new small prime p, the digit structure changes, so the analogous identity fails and the dilation trick breaks down.

But maybe both functions don't need full multiplicativity. In Tao's two-point argument, the multiplicativity of both λ functions at small primes lets you write λ(n)λ(n+1) = λ(pn)λ(pn+p) and then average over n while tracking the p | n events via entropy decrement. If only one function needs multiplicativity and the other just needs boundedness, that might open a path.        \"text\": \"Divisibility of $\\\\binom{2n}{n}$ by $n^\\\\ell$ has also been considered by several people; see the On-line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences [12], sequences A014847 $(\\\\ell=1)$ , A121943 $(\\\\ell=2)$ , A282163 $(\\\\ell=3)$ , A282346 (smallest n>1 with $n^\\\\ell | \\\\binom{2n}{n}, \\\\ell \\\\geqslant 1$ ), A282672 $(\\\\ell=6)$ , A283073 $(\\\\ell=4)$ , and A283074 $(\\\\ell=5)$ .\\n\\nOur main result is the following.\\n\\n**Theorem 1.** Fix $\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{N}$ . The set of n with $n^{\\\\ell} {2n \\\\choose n}$ has a positive asymptotic density $c_{\\\\ell}$ . The density may be computed as follows: Let $U_1, U_2, \\\\ldots$ be independent uniform-[0, 1] random variables, and let\\n\\n$$(1.1) g_1 = \\\\left\\\\lfloor \\\\frac{1}{U_1} \\\\right\\\\rfloor - 1, g_2 = \\\\left\\\\lfloor \\\\frac{1}{(1 - U_1)U_2} \\\\right\\\\rfloor - 1, \\\\dots, g_j = \\\\left\\\\lfloor \\\\frac{1}{(1 - U_1) \\\\cdots (1 - U_{j-1})U_j} \\\\right\\\\rfloor - 1, \\\\dots$$\\n\\nThen\\n\\n$$c_{\\\\ell} = \\\\mathbb{E} \\\\prod_{j=1}^{\\\\infty} \\\\left( 1 - 2^{-g_j} \\\\sum_{h=0}^{\\\\ell-1} {g_j \\\\choose h} \\\\right).$$\\n\\n\\n\\nis close to 1 when $g_j$ is substantially larger than $2\\\\ell$ and is close to 0 when $g_j$ is substantially smaller than $2\\\\ell$ . Thus, $c_\\\\ell$ should be close to the probability that $g_j \\\\geqslant 2\\\\ell$ for all j, which equals $\\\\rho(2\\\\ell)$ .\\n\\nA related problem is the study of the set $\\\\mathcal{B}$ of positive integers n such that n and $\\\\binom{2n}{n}$ are coprime, see e.g. sequence A082916 of the OEIS [12]. In [14], Sanna showed that $\\\\#(\\\\mathcal{B} \\\\cap [1, x]) \\\\ll x/\\\\sqrt{\\\\log x}$ for all\\n\\nx > 1. On the other hand, $\\\\mathcal{B}$ contains all odd primes, and thus $\\\\#(\\\\mathcal{B} \\\\cap [1, x]) \\\\ge (1 + o(1))x/\\\\log x$ for all $x \\\\ge 2$ . We sharpen these results by proving an asymptotic formula for $\\\\#(\\\\mathcal{B} \\\\cap [1, x])$ .\\n\\n**Theorem 3.** We have $\\\\#\\\\{n \\\\leqslant x : (n, \\\\binom{2n}{n}) = 1\\\\} \\\\sim cx/\\\\log x$ as $x \\\\to \\\\infty$ , where\",\n366:    \"thinking\": \" So problem 396 is still open, and Pomerance made real progress showing that for any k, there are infinitely many n where n\\u2212k divides the central binomial coefficient, plus the product of (n+1) through (n+k) divides it with density 1. That's the surprising part \\u2014 the upper shifts work out nicely, but the lower shifts including n itself are the real obstacle.\\n\\nThe asymmetry is striking: when p divides n+i, we get n \\u2261 \\u2212i mod p, forcing n mod p to be large (around p\\u2212i), which triggers a carry in the binary representation. But when p divides n\\u2212i, we get n \\u2261 i mod p with n mod p small \\u2014 no automatic carry. For the product \\u220f(n\\u2212i) to divide C(2n,n), every factor needs to be sqrt(2n)-smooth, meaning we need k+1 consecutive smooth numbers with the right digit structure, which is genuinely hard. Pomerance handled individual shifts, so the open question is whether the full product over consecutive integers below n divides infinitely often \\u2014 that's where the difficulty lies. Now I'm verifying the carry formula for binomial coefficients: when adding n + n in base p, a carry propagates from position j\\u22121 to j exactly when 2\\u00b7(n mod p^j) \\u2265 p^j, which gives the p-adic valuation as the count of positions where this inequality holds. For the necessary condition, if a prime p divides the product \\u220f(n\\u2212i) for i from 0 to k, then n \\u2261 i (mod p) for some i in that range. When p > 2k, we have n mod p = i < p/2, which means no carry occurs at the first digit position, and higher carries require examining the structure more carefully. This gives a cleaner necessary condition: for each prime power p^e dividing some n\\u2212i (with p > 2k), we need p^{2e} \\u2264 2n, and then the base-p digits of n must be at least p/2 at e different positions above position e. Now I'm working through the heuristic density for each prime, looking at how likely it is that among the digit positions up to log_p(2n), at least one satisfies the required threshold. There's also the smoothness constraint to factor in: each n\\u2212i needs to be sqrt(2n)-smooth, which happens with heuristic probability around 0.307 based on Dickman's function. For k+1 consecutive values, that's roughly 0.307^{k+1} accounting for correlations\\u2014positive but still raises the question of whether we can unconditionally prove infinitely many consecutive smooth numbers exist, which relates to classical results like Balog\\u2013Wooley's work on the topic.\\n\\nThe key insight is that Balog\\u2013Wooley established infinitely many strings of k consecutive integers that are each n^\\u03b5-smooth for any \\u03b5>0, so the smoothness obstruction alone is surmountable. But the real challenge is combining that with the digit conditions at medium primes and small-prime constraints simultaneously, layered on top of their explicit polynomial construction\\u2014which is actually promising since explicit constructions allow imposing additional congruence conditions. The density approach of showing positive lower density directly seems harder since even pairs of consecutive smooth numbers with positive density remains open, so the construction route through Balog\\u2013Wooley appears to be the most viable path forward.\\n\\nNow I'm checking whether Pomerance's density-1 result aligns with my asymmetry analysis, which would validate the framework. For the computational side, I need to verify and extend A375077 (the least n for each k), compute growth rates, identify which constraint binds most often for each k, and compare against heuristic density predictions. The first concrete theorem target is k=1: proving infinitely many n where n(n\\u22121) divides C(2n,n)\\u2014I should check if this is already known in the literature or if it's an open problem worth publishing. If open, the approach would involve ensuring both n and n\\u22121 are sqrt-smooth with appropriate digit conditions, possibly using special forms like n=m^2.\\n\\nThe key insight is that setting n=m^2 automatically makes n sqrt-smooth (largest prime factor \\u2264 m=\\u221an), and n\\u22121=(m\\u22121)(m+1) becomes a product of two ~\\u221an factors, so it's naturally (\\u221an+1)-smooth. This construction elegantly handles the smoothness requirement, and then I'd need to manage the carry conditions using polynomial-identity techniques like those Balog\\u2013Wooley generalize. For k=2, I'd extend this approach similarly. The radical condition\\u2014requiring all prime factors to divide the binomial coefficient\\u2014is the fundamental bottleneck, so meaningful relaxations involve either removing one exceptional index, shifting the range, or recentering the binomial coefficient itself by setting N = n + k and examining whether the product divides C(2N, N) instead.\\n\\nI'm also considering whether shifting N to be slightly larger than n, like N \\u2248 n + n^{1/2}, or dividing into binomial coefficients with adjusted parameters like C(2n, n\\u2212j) might offer more flexibility. Under standard smoothness conjectures, these approaches should yield positive density results.\\n\\nSince the problem only requires finding one witness n for each k, this is actually computationally tractable\\u2014each instance is verifiable in finite time, making the logical structure \\u03a02 but with decidable components. The next concrete step is to search for explicit witnesses n(k) for small values of k and see if there's a pattern in their growth.\\n\\nI should also verify the sequence A375077, which tracks the least n where the product \\u220f_{i=0}^{k}(n\\u2212i) divides C(2n,n), though I'll need to compute these values rather than assert them. Being honest about the difficulty: this remains an open Erd\\u0151s problem, so success on all ten cases is unlikely, but 396 is the strongest candidate because it decomposes well, connects to established machinery like Kummer's theorem and Pomerance's work, and has clear incremental milestones to verify.\",\n390:    \"signature\":    \"text\": \"# My pick: Problem 396\\n\\n**Is it true that for every $k$ there exists $n$ such that $\\\\prod_{0\\\\leq i\\\\leq k}(n-i) \\\\mid \\\\binom{2n}{n}$?**\\n\\nBefore justifying, I checked the problem page: it's still open, and the known partial results are exactly the kind that suggest a decomposable attack. Pomerance has shown that for any $k\\\\geq 0$ there are infinitely many $n$ such that $n-k\\\\mid\\\\binom{2n}{n}$, and that the set of $n$ such that $\\\\prod_{1\\\\leq i\\\\leq k}(n+i)\\\\mid \\\\binom{2n}{n}$ has density 1. So each individual factor is handled, and the *mirror-image* product (shifts above $n$) is essentially solved. The entire difficulty is concentrated in making the shifts *below* $n$ work simultaneously \\u2014 and as I'll show, there's a clean structural reason why, which immediately generates subproblems.\\n\\n## Why this one and not the other nine\\n\\nThe killer feature of 396 is that **Kummer's theorem converts it into a fully local, per-prime digit problem**, and the logical form (\\\"for every $k$ there exists $n$\\\") means each instance $k$ is finitely verifiable and a single construction suffices \\u2014 no density or finiteness statement needs proving. Compare:\\n\\n- **376, 730, 406**: all require controlling digit expansions in *multiple multiplicatively independent bases simultaneously* (3, 5, 7 for 376; base 3 vs. powers of 2 for 406). This is the same obstruction as Furstenberg-type \\u00d72/\\u00d73 problems \\u2014 there is essentially no known technique, and computation can only ever support the conjecture, never prove infinitude (376, 730) and never prove finiteness (406).\\n- **386**: an exponential Diophantine condition (binomial = product of consecutive primes); no local-to-global structure to decompose.\\n- **849**: Singmaster-adjacent. Exact multiplicity counts for every $t$ need constructions nobody has even conjecturally; small-$t$ search gives data but no path to \\\"for every $t$.\\\"\\n- **313**: solutions correspond exactly to primary pseudoperfect numbers (your A054377) \\u2014 only a handful are known, the search space explodes Sylvester-style, and infinitude looks as hard as Giuga-type problems.\\n- **18**: analytic, with the practical-number machinery giving few discrete footholds for experiment-driven progress.\\n- **1094**: a finiteness statement, so computation can't close it; needs effective Sylvester\\u2013Erd\\u0151s\\u2013Selfridge-type bounds.\\n- **1095**: my **second choice** \\u2014 \\\"estimate $g(k)$\\\" rewards partial progress (any improved bound is a result), and shares machinery with 396. Keep it as the pivot target.\\n\\n## The structural reduction (this is the engine of the whole plan)\\n\\nKummer: $v_p\\\\binom{2n}{n}$ = number of carries adding $n+n$ in base $p$, and a carry occurs out of position $j-1$ iff $n \\\\bmod p^j \\\\geq p^j/2$. So\\n\\n$$v_p\\\\binom{2n}{n} = \\\\#\\\\{j\\\\geq 1 : n \\\\bmod p^j \\\\geq p^j/2\\\\}.$$\\n\\nNow suppose $p \\\\mid n-i$ with $0\\\\leq i\\\\leq k$ and $p > 2k$. Then $n \\\\bmod p = i < p/2$: **no carry at the bottom digit**. If moreover $p^2 > 2n$, then $n \\\\bmod p^2 = n < p^2/2$, so no carries anywhere, hence $p \\\\nmid \\\\binom{2n}{n}$. This gives a hard **necessary condition**:\\n\\n> Every prime factor of every one of $n, n-1, \\\\dots, n-k$ must be $\\\\leq \\\\sqrt{2n}$. That is, you need $k+1$ **consecutive $\\\\sqrt{2n}$-smooth numbers** \\u2014 and on top of that, for each prime power $p^e \\\\| (n-i)$ you need $e$ carries among the base-$p$ digits *above* position $e$ (forcing $p^{2e} \\\\lesssim 2n$ plus digit conditions).\\n\\nThis also explains Pomerance's asymmetry in one line: $p \\\\mid n+i$ means $n \\\\equiv -i$, so $n \\\\bmod p = p-i \\\\geq p/2$ \\u2014 a free carry. The $+$ side is easy, the $-$ side requires consecutive smooth numbers with digit constraints. The good news: **infinitely many strings of $k$ consecutive $n^\\\\varepsilon$-smooth integers exist** by Balog\\u2013Wooley's explicit polynomial constructions, so the headline obstruction is matched by known technology. The open question becomes whether that construction has enough free parameters to absorb the carry conditions.\\n\\n## The breakdown\\n\\n**Tier A \\u2014 Foundations (lemma-level, each independently checkable)**\\n\\n1. Formalize the carry criterion above and prove the exact equivalence: $\\\\prod(n-i)\\\\mid\\\\binom{2n}{n}$ iff for every prime $p$, $\\\\sum_i v_p(n-i) \\\\leq \\\\#\\\\{j : n\\\\bmod p^j \\\\geq p^j/2\\\\}$.\\n2. So the smoothness obstruction vanishes *identically*, and the whole problem for $k=1$ reduces to: choose $m$ (with congruence/size freedom) so that the carry conditions hold for primes dividing $m, m-1, m+1$. ### THINKING
 Now I'm writing the main deliverable document and preparing three skeleton edits: updating Lemma B's header to reference the stronger TT theorem citations with pending verifications, upgrading the quantitative content in section (ii) while keeping T18 as a qualitative reference point, and revising the risk assessment at the end.

For the citations, I need to replace the vague Pilatte reference with precise theorem statements—specifically Theorem 1.1 on the Liouville function correlation bound and Theorem 2.4 on the multiplicative structure over prime bands, including the exact parameter setup with geometric spacing and the logarithmic bounds. The rest of the argument relies only on 1-boundedness rather than complete multiplicativity. The key spectral result constructs a large subset where the matrix has controlled eigenvalues using non-backtracking operators and spectral techniques from sections 4–13. There's also a remark about extending the two-point Chowla conjecture to almost all scales.

I'm extracting a general tool that applies to arbitrary 1-bounded functions, showing that certain weighted sums over divisors can be bounded by the spectral norm plus an exceptional contribution, where the bound decomposes into the restricted matrix action on the good set plus contributions from exceptional rows.

Now looking at a paper (arXiv:2512.01739) where section 1 gives the shape of a general estimate for shifted multiplicative functions: the average of a product involving two 1-bounded multiplicative functions with different shifts, restricted to residue classes, is bounded by a power of the logarithm outside a small exceptional set, with parameters bounded by logarithmic powers.

The key hypothesis on the first function is either real equidistribution (roughly constant mean across small modulus residue classes) or complex non-pretentiousness (quantified distance from Dirichlet characters times power functions). Theorem 1.7 then constructs an exceptional set of small logarithmic density where, outside it, the density of integers up to x that are simultaneously x^{1/u}-smooth and have x^{1/v}-smooth successors factors as the product of Dickman functions ρ(u)ρ(v) plus a logarithmic error term.

This strengthens the prior result by extending uniformity across a wider range of parameters u and v. The approach combines the circle method with additive-multiplicative structure (since ω is additive, the exponential sums e(αω(n)) remain multiplicative), uses sieve methods on major arcs in physical space, and relies on averaging techniques across scales as the current frontier. I need to confirm the exact quantified equidistribution definition, verify the asymmetry claim about the second function, and clarify the uniformity scope of the main theorem in auxiliary parameters.

For the lemma checklist, the key insight is that smoothness and band indicator functions are themselves multiplicative—they fit directly into the theorem's framework. The x-dependent thresholds match the uniformity structure in the earlier result, so I can either apply the main theorem if the hypotheses align, or adapt the derivation from the smooth-number application as a template. The next step is establishing equidistribution of these indicator functions in arithmetic progressions. The boundary between B and D hinges on whether anatomy indicators behave multiplicatively under dilation—they do, since dilation primes stay below the threshold where anatomy kicks in, making A(dn) = A(n) exactly. But digit conditions break this: dilation permutes residues modulo prime powers, preserving their distribution overall while destroying pointwise equality, and averaging over the dilation set randomizes individual digit values.

Three structural obstacles prevent extending the approach: dilation scrambles the digit condition's pointwise identity beyond repair; centering would require a new averaged-shift correlation estimate for multiplicative functions, which is plausible but unproven; and the digit functionals aren't additive like Ω, so the circle method can't encode them as multiplicative frequency objects. Some techniques may still survive the constraints. The dispersion-type bounds for the weighted exponential sum need to hold on average over moduli q, with logarithmic savings — this is the concrete target (Estimate D*) that replaces the parity approach. The key insight is that parity isn't actually a barrier here since our functions are non-negative with positive mean, so we're working in the equidistribution regime instead. For Theorem 1, I can use partial summation to convert the density count into an integral form that behaves asymptotically on logarithmic scales.

The realistic strategy is to establish estimates A, B, C, D at almost all scales simultaneously, which would deliver both the natural density version of Theorem 1 (with density c₁² plus logarithmic error terms, implying infinitude) and the log-density asymptotic form. The all-scales natural density version sits beyond the current frontier — even Tao-Teräväinen's EPS result has the same limitation — so this is the right target to pursue, not a compromise.

I need to work through the reading dependencies: C1, C2, C3 from Tao-Teräväinen's section 3 (when writing Lemma B), Pilatte's exceptional-row handling for the extracted 1-bounded tool if S1 gets used, and the averaged-shift correlations for F if I attempt a dilation-style approach. My next move is to fork into two tracks: start the manuscript with Lemma 0/0′ plus Lemma A and the new sub-lemma B0 (about 3–4 pages with minimal research risk), or pursue the k≥2 track with Bilu-Wintenberger witness carry profiles. I'm leaning toward the manuscript path since the reading session's output makes the B-layer write-up nearly mechanical, and drafting will force the C1–C3 verifications at the right moment.

The only other property of $\\lambda$ we\nuse is that it is 1-bounded.\"* Concretely:\n- **Tao's dilation** (2.4 ⟹ 1.1): $\\lambda(n)\\lambda(n+1)=\\lambda(dn)\\lambda(dn+d)$\n  for every $d\\in\\mathcal D$ — this *manufactures* the divisibility structure\n  $d\\mid m$ inside the shift-1 correlation. ### THINKING

### TEXT
The reading session is complete. Three verdicts, one structural bonus:

**Lemma B: upgraded to citable-in-principle.** The decisive find is Tao–Teräväinen's Theorem 1.7, which is our anatomy prototype *verbatim* — consecutive-smooth-pair asymptotic $\rho(u)\rho(v) + O(\log^{-c}x)$ at a log-density-1 set of scales, uniform in $u,v$ up to $c\log_2 x/\log_3 x$. That uniformity matters: it proves the framework tolerates $x$-dependent thresholds, which was our only nonstandard feature. And the deeper structural fact: our $z$-trick functions are *literally* multiplicative functions in their Theorem 3.1's class (equivalently, Tao's dilation works exactly for the anatomy layer, since dilation primes sit below $x^\delta$ where anatomy is blind). Remaining work is verification-grade — a half-page equidistribution lemma (B0) plus three fine-print checks against §3 when drafting — not research-grade.

**Lemma D: black-box definitively NO, with mechanisms.** Pilatte's own localization of where multiplicativity enters made this a clean diagnosis: (1) the dilation identity dies because digit residues transform by $r \mapsto (d \bmod p^j)r$ — and averaging over $d$ *randomizes the digits away*, destroying exactly what Lemma D is about; (2) the centring step consumes a multiplicative-functions input; (3) their frequency trick works because $\Omega$ is additive, and our digit functionals aren't. The technology boundary passes precisely between our Lemmas B and D.

**But the conceptual payoff cuts the other way: the parity barrier does not apply to Lemma D.** The entire Pilatte/entropy/spectral apparatus exists to break $\lambda$'s sign-sensitivity. Our indicators are non-negative, positive-mean, local conditions — D's true hardness class is large-moduli equidistribution on average (dispersion/BV, moduli to $x^{2/3+}$, divisor-correlated). Hard, but no known *wall* stands in front of it. D-i is now the lead attack, and D-ii is reformulated as a named target ("Estimate D\*" in the report). Bonus salvage: Pilatte's spectral engine uses only 1-boundedness, so it's available as a general bilinear hammer.

**Structural bonus:** almost-all-scales natural-density asymptotics imply the log-density asymptotic by partial summation — so proving all four lemmas in their *native* almost-all-scales mode yields both Theorem 1″ (infinitude with density $c_1^2$) and Theorem 1's log-density form simultaneously. Since even TT's own results carry the same all-scales limitation, this is the correct target, not a concession.

Everything is in **HYPOTHESES_CHECK.md** (engines stated precisely, the Lemma B checklist table, the D blockers, the reading-debt ledger), with the skeleton's statuses updated to match.

The fork now: **(a)** start the actual manuscript — Lemma 0/0′, Lemma A, and the new Lemma B0 written formally (~3–4 pages, zero research risk, and it forces the §3 fine-print checks at the right moment); or **(b)** the $k\ge2$ track — build Balog–Wooley witnesses and measure their carry profiles. I'd go (a): the reading made the B-layer nearly mechanical, and a partial manuscript is the right artifact to pressure-test the whole chain.

### THINKING
 The user wants me to step back and give a clear, honest summary of where things stand after all this technical work—what's proven, what's still open, and how the plan fits together. This is more about synthesis and explanation than diving into implementation details. The density conjecture for $k=1$ predicts that $W_1$ (pairs where both $n$ and $n-1$ divide the central binomial coefficient) should have density approximately $c_1^2 \approx 0.013$, where $c_1$ is the Ford–Konyagin constant, and the empirical evidence up to $10^9$ strongly supports this scaling. I've established a sandwich bound showing $W_1$ is trapped between two governor-based sets, verified exhaustively up to $60000$ and by sampling at $10^9$, with negligible margin cost.

The core insight is that membership in $D_0$ for consecutive integers $n$ and $n-1$ appears to behave independently, which would explain the squared density. I'm decomposing the condition "$n \in D_0$" into layers—small prime contributions, the anatomy of medium and large primes, digit and carry conditions given the prime structure on one side, and finally the joint digit conditions across both $n$ and $n-1$—and testing whether these independence assumptions hold empirically across the range up to $10^9$. Lemma D isn't amenable to black-box treatment, but I've pinned down its hardness as a large-moduli equidistribution problem in the BV/dispersion class rather than a parity barrier, which opens a path forward. The core strategy hinges on proving results at almost all scales, which simultaneously handles both the infinitude claim and the log-density asymptotics. For the main theorem with k=1, I'm laying out the lemmas: the sandwich bounds are done pending writeup, small primes can be handled via Friedlander-Iwaniec's approach, the anatomies of n and n-1are now independently distributed by the Tao-Teräväinen/Pilatte result, and the next step is conditioning on anatomy to complete the argument. The core challenge is proving that digit conditions hold simultaneously across consecutive integers — empirically it looks clean with no parity obstruction, and it reduces to dispersion estimates with moduli up to $x^{2/3}$ that divide $n-1$, pushing beyond the Bombieri–Vinogradov threshold where most problems hit a wall. It's genuinely unclear whether this needs a fundamentally new idea or whether careful localization of the conditions will crack it. Lemma C, by contrast, is established machinery from FK applied in a novel way. So the real question is whether infinitude of $W_1$ has actually been proven—I know empirically it contains 2, 210, 460, and shows roughly 1.1% density, but I need to check if Pomerance or others have established this rigorously. He does prove related divisibility results with positive density, which would support the claim, but I should verify the exact state of the literature before building the argument on it. sults in the literature, so I should be cautious about claiming it's definitively open — better to say "as far as our review found" rather than stating it as fact. The real challenge ahead is extending from the $k=1$ case to general $k$, which is the core Erdős problem. The skeleton approach generalizes this to $(k+1)$-point correlations, but the existing correlation technology from Pilatte and others is fundamentally designed for binary correlations.

The issue is that $k$-point correlations of multiplicative functions remain open even in restricted settings — log-averaged Chowla is still open for even $k \geq 4$. Our functions don't have parity constraints, so smoothness-type $k$-point correlations might be more tractable than the general case. Hildebrand's elementary methods worked for pairs, and while triples hit an obstruction with the stable-set method, that doesn't rule out the statement itself. The density approach for general $k$ would require $(k+1)$-point versions of the key lemmas, which gets progressively harder. Alternatively, there's the Balog–Wooley constructive route — building explicit witnesses with enough congruence freedom to force the carries — though that's a different direction entirely.

The actual plan sidesteps this: start with $k=1$ and the density theorem to build the machinery and get a first publishable result, then decide whether to extend the density machinery further or pivot to the Balog–Wooley construction for the general $k$ statement. I should also be clear about what success looks like at each stage: first, a complete write-up of the foundational lemmas and the reduction of pair density to the two open estimates — that's a solid preprint on its own. Then, if I can resolve the first open estimate, I get partial results toward the main theorem. Finally, resolving the second estimate completes the proof.

The first unconditional result would be infinitude and density of $n$ where $n(n-1)$ divides $\binom{2n}{n}$, with density $c_1^2$ at almost all scales plus logarithmic corrections. Beyond that, generalizing to arbitrary $k$ — the actual Erdős problem — requires either extending the density machinery to $(k+1)$-point correlations or using the Balog–Wooley construction.

The honest assessment: the second open estimate is where this either succeeds or stalls; everything else is technical work. The empirical evidence and barrier analysis suggest it's not hopeless — no parity obstruction, the conditions are local and positive — but dispersion beyond square-root moduli is exactly where analytic number theory typically hits a wall.

The realistic outcomes are: full resolution if the dispersion closes, which would be strong; a conditional reduction plus the first density result, still publishable; or the whole framework informs the constructive Balog–Wooley approach to the literal Erdős question. For context, the original problem asks whether witnesses exist for every $k$, which is a finite verification for each fixed $k$ (known up to $k=7$), but the real content is the "for all $k$" part — needing either a universal construction or a density argument that works across all $k$. The $k=1$ case is the template; pushing the density method to general $k$ would give far more than required, while the Balog–Wooley approach would give just existence, both relying on the carry and digit machinery being developed.

I need to be precise about the lemma statements in plain terms — particularly Lemma 0, which characterizes when $n(n-1) \mid \binom{2n}{n}$ in terms of membership in Pomerance's governor set $D_0$ (the set of $m$ where $m \mid \binom{2m}{m}$), which has known density around $0.114$ from Ford–Konyagin. The sandwich involves intersecting $D_0$ with a shifted version, and I need to state that carefully without losing the reader.

The key insight is that the carry conditions for $n-1$'s primes depend on the digits of $n$, but those digits coincide with $n-1$'s digits up to a small margin in each residue class. The margin sets differ from $D_0$ by a negligible density (at most $10^{-4}$, empirically closer to $6 \times 10^{-5}$ at $10^9$), so the whole theorem reduces to showing that the events "$n \in D_0$" and "$n-1 \in D_0$" are asymptotically independent.

Now I'm unpacking what membership in $D_0$ actually means: for each prime power dividing $m$, I need enough carries among the relevant digit positions. The smoothness condition $P^+(m) \le \sqrt{2m}$ is necessary, and for medium-sized primes the carry conditions behave like random coin flips on the digits. I'm also setting up two key lemmas—one showing that small primes contribute negligibly, and another analyzing how the "anatomies" (the distribution of prime sizes) behave across the two consecutive integers.

The core technical challenge is showing that the digit conditions at $n$'s own primes equidistribute uniformly when weighted multiplicatively, and then proving independence between the digit conditions on the two sides. I should also lay out the full theorem hierarchy: the main asymptotic result with density $c_1^2$, a version covering almost all scales as the realistic target, and a fallback using weaker tools for positive lower density.

One clarification worth emphasizing: for $k=1$, the literal Erdős question is trivial since $n=2$ works, but our theorem addresses the density and infinitude of simultaneous divisibility cases—a model problem that apparently remains unproven even for infinitude alone.
