### TEXT
Good moment to zoom out. Let me lay it out from the top, plainly.

## What we're actually trying to prove

The Erdős problem asks: for every $k$, is there *some* $n$ with $(n)(n-1)\cdots(n-k) \mid \binom{2n}{n}$? For $k=1$ the literal question is trivial ($n=2$ works), and witnesses are known computationally up to $k=7$. The mathematical content is the "for every $k$" — you need either a construction or a counting argument that works uniformly.

The program's bet: prove the **density version for $k=1$ first** — that the set $W_1 = \{n : n(n-1) \mid \binom{2n}{n}\}$ has density $c_1^2 \approx 0.013$, where $c_1 = 0.11424$ is the Ford–Konyagin constant for the single-condition set $D_0 = \{m : m \mid \binom{2m}{m}\}$. As far as our literature review found, even *infinitude* of $W_1$ is not in the literature, so this is a real theorem target, and it's the model case that builds all the machinery for general $k$. Empirically it's clearly true: measured pair density at $10^9$ is $0.92 \times c_1^2$ (finite-scale), converging.

## The skeleton, rung by rung

**Reduction 1 (done, verified).** Kummer's theorem makes everything local: $p$-divisibility of $\binom{2n}{n}$ counts carries, i.e. digit conditions $2(n \bmod p^j) \ge p^j$. **Lemma 0** then sandwiches $W_1$ between $\{n \in D_0\} \cap \{n-1 \in D_0^{(\pm)}\}$, where the margin sets $D_0^{(\pm)}$ differ from Pomerance's governor set $D_0$ by measured density $\le 6\times10^{-5}$. So Theorem 1 becomes exactly: **"the events $n \in D_0$ and $n-1 \in D_0$ are asymptotically independent."** Ford–Konyagin already proved the single event has density $c_1$; we need the pair to factorize.

**Reduction 2 (the four-lemma factorization).** Membership in $D_0$ has two layers: the *anatomy* of $n$ (the sizes of its large prime factors — in particular $\sqrt{2n}$-smoothness) and the *digit conditions* (given the primes, do the carries land right — roughly fair coins at medium primes). Independence of the pair splits into:

- **Lemma A** — primes below $n^\delta$ are ignorable, both sides. Adapting Ford–Konyagin's own §2. *Low risk, ready to write.*
- **Lemma B** — the anatomies of $n$ and $n-1$ are independent. This is "consecutive smooth numbers" technology. **The big news from the reading session: this is now essentially citable.** Tao–Teräväinen (Dec 2025) prove the smooth-pair asymptotic $\rho(u)\rho(v)$ quantitatively at almost all scales, and our indicator functions are literally multiplicative functions in their theorem's class. What remains is a half-page lemma and three fine-print checks.
- **Lemma C** — given the anatomy, the digit conditions on $n$'s *own* primes equidistribute, uniformly against any multiplicative weight living on $n-1$. This is Ford–Konyagin's hardest section, re-run with a twist. *Medium risk: known-hard technology in a new combination.* Our tests showed the quantitative reality here: a real ~4% finite-scale within-side deviation that their theorem covers asymptotically but any error bookkeeping must beat.
- **Lemma D** — the digit conditions of the two sides are independent *of each other*. **This is the hard core and the genuinely open mathematics.** Empirically immaculate (ratios 0.984/1.003/0.995 across three independent runs).

**Assembly.** A × B × C × D with error bookkeeping gives Theorem 1″ (density $c_1^2 + O(\log^{-c}x)$ at almost all scales), which we showed implies both infinitude and the log-density asymptotic. "Almost all scales" is the honest target — even Tao–Teräväinen's own results carry that limitation; it's the field's frontier, not our concession.

## The main open questions, ranked

**1. Lemma D — where the program succeeds or stalls.** Everything else is engineering; this needs new mathematics. The reading session's contribution was a precise diagnosis: the Pilatte/Tao–Teräväinen machinery *cannot* be cited for it (their methods need multiplicativity in exactly the two places our digit conditions break it), **but the parity barrier — the famous wall behind Chowla-type problems — does not apply to us**, because our conditions are non-negative, positive-mean, and local. Lemma D's true difficulty class is *equidistribution in arithmetic progressions to large moduli on average* — dispersion-type estimates with moduli up to $x^{2/3}$ that divide $n-1$. That's beyond the Bombieri–Vinogradov range, which is exactly where analytic number theory routinely gets stuck — so this could need a real idea, or could yield to careful dispersion because our conditions are so structured. Unknown until tried. We've at least formulated the precise estimate needed ("Estimate D\*").

**2. Lemma C.** Less scary but not free: it requires multiplicative-functions-in-progressions inputs averaged over large moduli, plus the top-digit deterministic law the tests uncovered.

**3. The bridge to general $k$** — the actual Erdős question. The skeleton generalizes to $(k+1)$-point correlations, but binary correlation technology is the current frontier ($k$-point versions are open even for smoothness). The alternative track is Balog–Wooley's explicit constructions (build witnesses with enough congruence freedom to force the carries) — parked, but everything built here (the carry calculus, Lemma A, the digit laws) transfers to it.

**Honest calibration of outcomes:** (i) best case, dispersion closes Lemma D → first unconditional theorem: density of $n$ with $n(n-1)\mid\binom{2n}{n}$; (ii) middle case, C done but D resists → a clean conditional paper: "pair density $= c_1^2$ modulo one precisely-stated equidistribution estimate," plus the verified sandwich and Lemma A/B; (iii) worst case, the machinery feeds the constructive track for the literal Erdős statement. Rung (ii) is already locked in as achievable — Lemmas 0, A, B are between done and citable — which is why my recommendation is to start writing the manuscript now and let it force the remaining verifications in order.

The Kloosterman machinery and error bounds in averaged forms naturally handle character-twisted sums, which is closest to what we need. The gap is our weight decoration plus the extra twist $e(ht/q)$ representing frequency $h/q$—this relates to evaluating at a major arc with large denominator. Crucially, de la Bretèche–Drappeau's work on friable exponential sums directly studies $\sum_{n \text{ friable}} e(an/q)$-type expressions, and they have explicit cancellation estimates for $y$-smooth $n \le x$ that should apply here.

If we can access a result bounding such sums uniformly for $q$ up to $x^{1/2}$, then removing the $n$-side weight entirely—leaving just the pure $(t-1)$-side equidistribution with $\sum_t \text{smooth}, e(ht/q)$ and no $F(qt+1)$ factor—becomes citable. The coupling through $F(qt+1)$ is the genuinely new contribution, and Pilatte's spectral approach might serve as a bilinear tool to handle the $q$-averaged form after applying Cauchy.

For the off-diagonal piece, I'm looking at sums over primes $q \sim Q$ of exponential sums $e(hm/q)$, where the phase varies slowly as $q$ changes. By van der Corput, if the second derivative is large enough—roughly when $m \gg Q^2/h$—we get oscillation and cancellation. Since $m = u(v_1 - v_2)$ ranges up to around $UV^2$ while $Q^2 \sim x^{2\gamma}$, I need to check whether this regime actually gives us useful cancellation.

The Fourier-in-the-modulus approach works here because the exponential sums behave like a smooth real-valued function as $q$ varies over primes, which I can extract via the prime number theorem and van der Corput. I could also try to carry along the diagonal-adjacent terms.

### THINKING
 I'm diving into Lemma D now—the genuinely difficult part of this problem. Let me first clarify exactly what Lemma D states by reviewing the current setup: $W_1$ is defined as the set of integers $n$ where $n(n-1)$ divides $\binom{2n}{n}$, and using Kummer's theorem plus Lemma 0, I can characterize membership in $W_1$ in terms of conditions on $n$ being in $D_0$ along with some additional constraints up to negligible error margins.

The key insight is that $D_0$ itself is characterized by a carry-counting condition: for each prime power $p^e$ dividing $m$, the number of carry positions $\kappa_p(m)$ when doubling $m$ in base $p$ must be at least $e$. The tricky part is that when checking whether both $n$ and $n-1$ satisfy these conditions, I need to be careful about which base I'm working in—the digit conditions for $n$ use bases dividing $n$, while those for $n-1$ use bases dividing $n-1$.

The four-lemma decomposition breaks down the problem: Lemma A handles small primes as negligible, Lemma B establishes that the prime anatomies of $n$ and $n-1$ are independent (via the Turán-type result), Lemma C shows that conditional on both anatomies, the digit conditions on the $n$-side equidistribute uniformly even against bounded multiplicative weights on the $n-1$ side, and now Lemma D is asserting that the digit conditions on both sides are jointly independent—meaning the $n-1$ side still equidistributes even after conditioning on the anatomy and the digit outcome of the $n$ side.

To make this rigorous, I need to show that the correlation $\sum_{n \le x} F_1(n) F_2(n-1)$ factors as the product of the individual means times $x$ plus lower-order terms, where $F_i$ encode both the anatomy and digit-condition information. The strategy is to expand the $n-1$ conditions by isolating the band primes (those in the range $((2n)^{1/3}, \sqrt{2n}]$) and their carry structure, then use the factorization $n-1 = qm$ to separate the digit condition from the prime's contribution.

This count is nonzero only when $p_1 \equiv p_2 \bmod q$, which severely restricts the sum.

The issue is that the phase cancellation gets lost in this approach—the exponential factors have modulus 1 and don't interfere constructively. Instead, I should fix a single prime $p$ and move the deep phase cancellation into the sum over residues $a$, creating a weighted Fermat-quotient exponential sum over integers $a$ up to $x^{1-u}$ restricted to a specific residue class modulo $q$.

Now I'm checking whether the sum length $x^{1-u}$ is actually shorter than the period $q^2$, which ranges from $x^{2/3}$ to $x$. For certain parameter choices like $u = u' = 0.4$, the sum is indeed shorter than the period, which puts us in the regime where Bourgain–Konyagin bounds apply to these Fermat-quotient sums.

This connects to work by Bourgain, Konyagin, and Shparlinski on exponential sums with Fermat quotients—I'm trying to recall the exact threshold, whether it's $N \ge q^{1/2+\epsilon}$ or $N \ge q^{1+\epsilon}$, but the key point is that the smooth structure of $a$ (as a cofactor with multiplicative constraints) allows us to factor it further and exploit its smooth-number properties.

Since $a$ is smooth, we can write it as a product of small primes, and because the Fermat quotient is additive—it's a group homomorphism from $(\mathbb{Z}/q^2)^*$ to $\mathbb{Z}/q$—the phase becomes linear in logarithmic coordinates. This means sums over products factor completely, and we get Vinogradov–Vaughan-style decompositions essentially for free. The real challenge now is understanding how the image of this homomorphism distributes.

But there's another angle we haven't exploited yet: averaging over $q$ itself. Using Cauchy–Schwarz on the error terms and looking at cross-terms between different moduli, we'd need to handle pairs of congruence conditions modulo $(q_1 q_2)^2$, which quickly becomes too large relative to $x$ for the counting argument to work cleanly.

The character sum approach is more tractable here. For this session, I want to nail down the reduction theorem rigorously—specifically, showing that Lemma D reduces to a sharp estimate on character-twisted bilinear sums involving Fermat quotients, with the precise form of what we need to prove spelled out clearly.

I also need to search the literature for what's known unconditionally about Fermat quotient exponential sums and related work by Bourgain–Konyagin–Shparlinski, Heath-Brown, and Shkredov on Heilbronn sums, plus whether anyone's tackled primes in residue classes modulo p using these techniques.

Then I'll build a numerology table to see which band pairs $(u, u')$ can be handled by existing Fermat quotient bounds, pinpointing the critical region. Finally, I need to test the core prediction empirically: whether Fermat quotients equidistribute cleanly over band primes and smooth numbers—if that distribution is messy, the whole approach fails; if it's clean, that's a strong signal the reduction works.

I should also directly measure the error terms from the character sum analysis on real data at scale $10^9$ to verify they behave like square-root bounds, which is a new kind of validation we haven't run before. And I'll sanity-check the reduction on small cases first, then carefully verify the digit-carry condition for the $(n-1)$-side at band primes. The framework pins down the condition $m \bmod q \in [q/2, q)$ exactly, with determinism emerging from the joint distribution of $q$ and $n$'s size — this gets handled automatically through exact counting rather than heuristics. For the error term in Lemma D, I just need to verify that character sums over deep characters stay small. Now I'm checking that characters modulo $q^2$ decompose as a product of the lifted mod-$q$ character and a deeper character component.

I'm verifying the factorization of $(\mathbb{Z}/q^2)^*$ and confirming how characters act on the Teichmüller lifts versus the $1 + q\mathbb{Z}$ part, then checking whether the deep character can be expressed using the Fermat quotient $\ell_q(n) = \frac{n^{q-1} - 1}{q} \bmod q$. Now I'm connecting this to Heilbronn sums and how they relate to complete character sums with conductors—the Gauss sum bound gives us magnitude $q$. The Heilbronn sum itself appears when summing over the Teichmüller subgroup, which matters for dual formulations and counting problems like bounding zeros of Fq. But for the main application, I need incomplete sums of the form $\sum_{n \le N} e(\lambda\text{Fq}(n)/q)$ and their variants over primes or smooth numbers, which is where Bourgain–Konyagin–Shparlinski come in.

I should also note that higher exponents $e \ge 2$ (where $q^2 \| n - 1$) with multiple band primes per side create finitely many configurations that fit the same framework—just conditions modulo $q^{e+1}$ with deeper characters—so those reduce to bookkeeping. The trickier question is whether Lemma D's bilinear form requires deep phases on both the $n$-side and the $n-1$-side simultaneously, since the full statement has digit conditions on both sides.

When I parametrize $n = pa$ and impose the $(n-1)$ conditions via characters, the $n$-side digit condition becomes a condition on $a \bmod p$, which gets embedded into the weight $v$ on the $a$-variable. But this means $v(a)$ loses multiplicativity since it now depends jointly on $a \bmod p$ and $p$ itself. For the Cauchy–Schwarz moves in $a$ that's manageable since $v$ stays bounded, but when factoring out smooth variables, the $a \bmod p$ condition can be Fourier-expanded using classical characters at level $p$.

The cleaner approach is to structure the proof conditionally: Lemma C already covers the "$n$-side digits times multiplicative weight on $n-1$" case, so Lemma D's new content is just handling the $(n-1)$-side deep layer against the $n$-side-decorated weight—which is exactly the $E_q$ object where $v$ incorporates the $n$-side digit condition as a bounded, non-multiplicative but mod-$p$-structured weight. This reduces to proving deep-character equidistribution for the fixed $n$-side-decorated sequence.

Now I'm searching for relevant literature, starting with papers on Bourgain, Konyagin, Shparlinski, and Fermat quotients in exponential sums. For the sample band primes in the range around 1e3 to 1e4, the subpopulations within each residue class modulo q are large enough to detect meaningful cancellation—we're looking at inner populations of 10^4 to 10^5, which gives us enough signal to distinguish between the trivial bound and actual sqrt-like cancellation. Now I'm setting up the computation of the character sum $T_q(\lambda)$ over these populations. Before committing to this approach, I should check whether there's a theoretical obstruction I'm overlooking—particularly around the diagonal issue in $E_q$. When I fold the main term subtraction with $\lambda = 0$ using the exact FK piecewise law, I need to verify that the condition on $m \bmod q$ really is independent of the size relation, especially when dealing with the interval $[q/2, q)$ and the constraint $2(n-1 \bmod q^2) \ge q^2$.

The key insight is that this condition does reduce exactly to the interval condition on $m \bmod q$—the "top-digit determinism" emerges because when $q^2 \approx n$, the value $m = (n-1)/q$ stays close to $n/q$ and isn't much larger than $q$ itself, so $m \bmod q$ gets constrained by $m$'s size rather than distributing freely. In the harmonic framework, when summing over $n \sim x$ with $q | n-1$ at the top of the band, the range of $m$ has length roughly $x/q \lesssim q$, which is shorter than the modulus—making equidistribution impossible due to these size constraints.

For the error analysis, I need to compute the main term as an exact count using the Farey-Kloosterman floor formula, and the error sums over $m$ in intervals that might be shorter than $q$. At the deep layer where the phase is $e(\lambda m/q)$, a short linear exponential sum over $m$-intervals smaller than $q$ is bounded by $\min(\text{length}, 1/\|\lambda/q\|)$ without needing per-interval cancellation—the cancellation must instead come from the structure of $p$ and $a$ modulo $q$. The oscillation of $e(\lambda m/q)$ works fine as long as $\lambda \cdot (x/q) / q \gg 1$, which gives enough phase variation across the range. The issue is that m isn't summed smoothly since it's tied to the multiplicative structure through the relation $1 + qm = pa$. So while the framework is coherent, the real challenge lies in handling bilinear forms with Fourier-type phases. I need to be realistic: this reduction transforms Lemma D into Fourier-type sums over primes and smooth numbers (possibly with mod-q constraints), where some unconditional techniques exist from prior work, and the bilinear structure offers flexibility that generic dispersion methods don't have. Whether current bounds cover the ranges I need is an open question—some parts of the band might be covered, others might not.

The key insight emerging is that for a fixed prime p, the map sending q to the character value $\ell_q(p)$ behaves very randomly across different q's, similar to Wieferich heuristics. A genuine large sieve averaged over (q, λ) pairs tailored to this bilinear structure could potentially close the entire gap—this is the main theoretical question I should flag from this work. The natural next step would be to attempt a q-averaged large sieve computation, which would give a mean-value theorem for deep characters. $^2 \mid n_1^{q-1} - n_2^{q-1}$ when $(n_i, q) = 1$, which translates to the Frobenius character condition $\text{Fq}(n_1\bar n_2) = 0$ — this means $(n_1\bar n_2)^{q-1} \equiv 1 \pmod{q^2}$, and I need to work out what this looks like in integer terms.

For the diagonal case where $n_1 = n_2$, the sum over primes $q$ weighted by $q$ gives roughly $\frac{Q^2}{2\log Q}\|a\|^2$. The off-diagonal terms are trickier — for each fixed pair $n_1 \ne n_2$, I'm counting how many primes $q \sim Q$ satisfy the divisibility condition, which is essentially a generalized Wieferich prime problem. This is unprovable pointwise (like the original Wieferich conjecture), but averaging over all pairs might give something tractable.

Now I'm reformulating the off-diagonal sum by switching the order: instead of fixing $n_1, n_2$ and counting primes, I'm fixing $q$ and counting pairs $(n_1, n_2)$ where $n_1 \equiv t n_2 \pmod{q^2}$ for some $t$ in the Teichmüller subgroup. This reduces to a multiplicative energy problem between an interval and a subgroup, which I can estimate using Heilbronn-type sums to get $E_q \approx \frac{N^2(q-1)}{\phi(q^2)}$.

Now I'm switching to character-theoretic methods to handle the indicator function for membership in $T_q$: using the fact that $T_q$ is the kernel of the Frobenius map, I can express the indicator via a character sum, which will let me decompose $E_q$ into a main term plus error terms controlled by non-trivial characters. So the character sum bound extends to the full character group since we're only adding nonnegative terms, giving us $\sum_{\lambda \ne 0}|S(\chi_\lambda)|^2 \le \phi(q^2) \cdot N$ for $N \le q^2$. This yields the unconditional bound $E_q \ll N^2/q + qN$ for the off-diagonal Wieferich-pair count at fixed $q$.

Summing over all moduli with the large sieve, the off-diagonal contribution becomes $\sum_q q(E_q - N) \ll N^2 \pi(Q) + Q^3 N$, which when normalized gives a total large sieve bound of roughly $(Q^2/\log Q + QN/\log Q + Q^3/\log Q)\|a\|_2^2$ for sequences on $[N]$—though I need to check how this compares to the diagonal term $Q^2 N$.

For indicator functions with $\|a\|^2 = N$, the per-character average of $|S|^2$ works out to $(N + N^2/Q + QN)/\log Q$, which is larger than the square-root cancellation bound of $N$ by the extra $N^2/Q$ term—but this becomes harmless when $N \le x^{2/3}$ and $Q$ is appropriately bounded.

The simple least squares approach gives an average $|S|$ that's roughly $\sqrt{QN}$ with a saving factor of $\sqrt{N/Q}$ over the trivial bound, which is nontrivial whenever $N > Q^{1+\epsilon}$. For the $a$-variable case where $N = x^{1-u}$ and $Q = x^{u'}$, we're at the marginal corner but still get a nontrivial saving of $x^{(1-u-u')/2}$.

For the $p$-variable, the saving is nontrivial only when $u > u'$, covering half the band. Combined with bilinearity and the total saving needed, these soft unconditional tools—subgroup orthogonality and positivity—do yield genuine nontrivial improvements across parts of the parameter range. The goal is to map out systematically what's covered by the soft least squares approach, what's already in the Fourier-analytic literature, and what gaps remain. I should verify the positivity step more carefully though.

The large-sieve direction applies to the averaged object over $q$, which is fine. ### TEXT
Lemma D it is. The reading session left us with the diagnosis — D's hardness class is large-moduli equidistribution, parity-free — but "Estimate D\*" was still stated in generic dispersion language. My plan for this session: drive the reduction all the way down to its true harmonic core, identify what that core *is* in the literature, prove whatever falls out unconditionally, and test the key cancellation empirically.

Let me start with the structural move I've been circling. The $(n-1)$-side digit condition at a band prime $q$ is exactly: $n \equiv 1 \pmod q$ **and** $(n-1)/q \bmod q \in [q/2, q)$ — i.e., $n$ lies in specific classes mod $q^2$ *above* $1 \bmod q$. The group $(\mathbb{Z}/q^2)^* \cong \mathbb{Z}/(q{-}1) \times \mathbb{Z}/q$, and characters detecting the mod-$q^2$ refinement of a mod-$q$ class are precisely the **depth-1 characters** $\chi_\lambda(n) = e(\lambda\,\ell_q(n)/q)$, where $\ell_q$ is the **Fermat quotient** — a group homomorphism, so it factors completely over our multiplicative structure $n = pa$. That would turn Lemma D into Fermat-quotient exponential sums — named, studied objects. Before building on this, let me check what's actually known about them.
